  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_abc2c713-66e9-431e-be25-eb54f68137c0'
      });

<span id="freeTextContainer3540609828056719220">A classic of Mexican modern literature about a haunted village.<br/><br/>As one enters Juan Rulfo's legendary novel, one follows a dusty road to a town of death. Time shifts from one consciousness to another in a hypnotic flow of dreams, desires, and memories, a world of ghosts dominated by the figure of Pedro Páramo - lover, overlord, murderer.<br/><br/>Rulfo's extraordinary mix of sensory i</span>
  <span id="freeText3540609828056719220" style="display:none">A classic of Mexican modern literature about a haunted village.<br /><br />As one enters Juan Rulfo's legendary novel, one follows a dusty road to a town of death. Time shifts from one consciousness to another in a hypnotic flow of dreams, desires, and memories, a world of ghosts dominated by the figure of Pedro Páramo - lover, overlord, murderer.<br /><br />Rulfo's extraordinary mix of sensory images, violent passions, and unfathomable mysteries has been a profound influence on a whole generation of Latin American writers, including Carlos Fuentes, Mario Vargas Llosa, and Gabriel García Márquez. To read Pedro Páramo today is as overwhelming an experience as when it was first published in Mexico back in 1955.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3540609828056719220" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

- Déjà morte ? Et de quoi ?
- Je ne sais pas. Peut-être de tristesse. Elle soupirait tout le temps.

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6431467-s-penkevich?shelf=mike_puma_made_me_do_it">mike_puma_made_me_do_it</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6431467-s-penkevich?shelf=mexico">mexico</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6431467-s-penkevich?shelf=death_and_decay">death_and_decay</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer856056618" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6074223452417865233">‘<i>The sun was tumbling over things, giving them form once again. The ruined, sterile earth lay before him.</i>’<br/><br/>There are passages of Juan Rulfo’s exquisite ‘<i>Pedro Páramo</i>’ that I want to cut out and hang upon my walls like a valuable painting. Because that is what this novel is, a purely beautiful surrealistic painting of a hellish Mexico where words are the brushstrokes and the ghastly, ghostly tone is the color palate. Rulfo’s short tale is an utter masterpiece, and the forerunner of magical realism</span>
  <span id="freeText6074223452417865233" style="display:none">‘<i>The sun was tumbling over things, giving them form once again. The ruined, sterile earth lay before him.</i>’<br><br>There are passages of Juan Rulfo’s exquisite ‘<i>Pedro Páramo</i>’ that I want to cut out and hang upon my walls like a valuable painting. Because that is what this novel is, a purely beautiful surrealistic painting of a hellish Mexico where words are the brushstrokes and the ghastly, ghostly tone is the color palate. Rulfo’s short tale is an utter masterpiece, and the forerunner of magical realism¹—a dark swirling fog of surrealism and horror that is both simple and weightless, yet weighs heavy like an unpardonable sin upon the readers heart and soul. <blockquote>
  <i>Nights around here are filled with ghosts. You should see all the spirits walking through the streets. As soon as it is dark they begin to come out. No one likes to see them. There’s so many of them and so few of us that we don’t even make the effort to pray for them anymore, to help them out of their purgatory. We don’t have enough prayers to go around…Then there are our sins on top of theirs. None of us still living is in God’s grace. We can’t lift up our eyes, because they’re filled with shame.</i>
</blockquote>When Juan Preciado visit’s his mother’s home of Comala to his father, the long deceased and ‘<i>pure bile</i>’ of a man, Pedro Páramo, he finds a town of rot and decay filled with ghosts, both figuratively and literally. This is a place of utter damnation, where the sins of a family are so strong that their bloodstained hands have tainted and tarnished the immortal souls of all they come in contact with, leaving in their wake a trail of withered, writhing spirits condemned to forever inhabit their hellish homes. There is nothing pleasant—aside from the intense, striking poetry of Rulfo’s words—to be found in the history of Comala, a town burdened by a list of sins so long and dark that even the preacher’s soul cannot escape from the vile vortex.<blockquote>
  <i>Life is hard as it is. The only thing that keeps you going is the hope that when you die you’ll be lifted off this mortal coil; but when they close one door to you and the only one left open is the door to Hell, you’re better off not being born…</i>
</blockquote>This violent, vitriolic landscape forges an unforgettable portrait of Rulfo’s Mexico, eternally encapsulating his vision into the glorious dimensions of myth. The small novel reads like a bedtime story meant to instill good morality in children through fear, while still enchanting their mind’s eye with a disintegrating stage furnished by crumbling, cadaverous buildings and populated by doomed phantoms. His style is phenomenal, effortlessly swapping between past and present, character to character, all in order to build a montage of madness and damnation.<br><br>Rulfo’s book is easily digested in a sitting or two, yet will nourish (or cling like a parasite to) your literary soul for an eternity. A dazzling surrealism coupled with a simple, yet potent prose make this an unforgettable classic, and one that has inspired many great authors since its first printing. A hellish portrait of society, brilliantly incorporating political events to help illustrate an abominable image of the dark side of Mexican history, Rulfo immortalizes himself and his homeland into myth and legend. A must read that will haunt you like the pale specters whose voices echo forever in the streets of Comala.<br><b>4.5/5</b><br><br>‘<i>This town is filled with echoes. It's like they were trapped behind the walls, or beneath the cobblestones. When you walk you feel like someone's behind you, stepping in your footsteps. You hear rustlings. And people laughing. Laughter that sounds used up. And voices worn away by the years.</i>’ <br><br>¹ <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/13450.Gabriel_Garc_a_M_rquez" title="Gabriel García Márquez" rel="nofollow noopener">Gabriel Garcí­a Márquez</a>, who once said of Rulfo’s novel ‘I could recite the whole book, forwards and backwards,’ (<a href="http://www.slate.com/articles/arts/books/2008/03/the_perfect_novel_youve_never_heard_of.2.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Rediscovering Pedro Páramo</a>), credits the book as playing a major chord of inspiration in his brand of ‘magical realism’. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1392525023i/8573343.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="6074223452417865233" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer12404199985242283780"><b>Pedro Páramo = Pedro Paramo (1955), Juan Rulfo</b><br/><br/>Pedro Paramo is a novel written by Juan Rulfo about a man named Juan Preciado who travels to his recently deceased mother's hometown, Comala, to find his father, only to come across a literal ghost town─populated, that is, by spectral figures. <br/><br/>Paramo was a key influence on Latin American writers such as Gabriel García Márquez. Gabriel García Márquez has said that he felt blocked as a novelist after writing his first four books and that it was only hi</span>
  <span id="freeText12404199985242283780" style="display:none"><b>Pedro Páramo = Pedro Paramo (1955), Juan Rulfo</b><br /><br />Pedro Paramo is a novel written by Juan Rulfo about a man named Juan Preciado who travels to his recently deceased mother's hometown, Comala, to find his father, only to come across a literal ghost town─populated, that is, by spectral figures. <br /><br />Paramo was a key influence on Latin American writers such as Gabriel García Márquez. Gabriel García Márquez has said that he felt blocked as a novelist after writing his first four books and that it was only his life-changing discovery of Pedro Páramo in 1961 that opened his way to the composition of his masterpiece, One Hundred Years of Solitude. <br /><br />Moreover, García Márquez claimed that he <i>"could recite the whole book, forwards and backwards."</i> Jorge Luis Borges considered Pedro Paramo to be one of the greatest texts written in any language. <br /><br />Pedro Paramo has been translated into more than 30 different languages and the English version has sold more than a million copies in the United States.<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز شانزدهم ماه 1990میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: پدرو پارامو؛ نویسنده: خوان رولفو؛ مترجم: احمد گلشیری؛ چاپ نخست: تهران، کتاب تهران، 1363؛ در 133ص؛ چاپ دیگر اصفهان، نشر فردا، 1371؛ موضوع داستانهای نویسندگان مکزیکی - سده 20م<br /><br />عنوان: پدرو پارامو؛ نویسنده: خوان رولفو؛ مترجم: کیومرث پارسای؛ تهران، چلچله، 1395؛ در 176ص؛ شابک 9789649027081؛<br /><br />عنوان: پدرو پارامو؛ نویسنده: خوان رولفو؛ مترجم: کاوه میرعباسی؛ تهران، ماهی، 1395؛ در 176ص؛ شابک 9789649042091؛<br /><br />در داستان «پدرو پارامو»، مردی بنا بر وصیت مادرش، در جستجوی ردی از پدر هرگز ندیده ی خویش «پدرو پارامو»، راهی سفر میشود؛ پدری که زندگی اش، همانند محتویات صندوق «پاندورا»ست؛ مقصد سفر، جایی به نام «کومالا»؛ دهکده ای متروک است، که ارواح ساکنان پیشینش، در آنجا در گشت و گذار هستند، شاید با تکیه بر همین برهان ـ و به تناسب آشفتگیهای عالم ارواح ـ است، که نویسنده، شیوه ی روایت عجیب و پیچیده ای را برای نگارش داستان خویش برگزیده؛ شیوه ای که باعث میشود خوانشگر در جای جای داستان نه چندان بلند، چندین بار با دیگر شدن «موضوع»، «راوی»، و «زمان» روبرو شود<br /><br />تاریخ بهنگام رسانی 03/08/1399هجری خورشیدی؛ ا. شربیانی</span>

She said you’d arrive today.”<br/>“My mother…my mother is dead.”<br/>“Oh, then that’s why her voice sounded so weak.” <br/><br/>This book, really a novella (120 pages), is a Mexican classic, an early example of magical realism. It’s original, startling, unique. According to Wikipedia Gabriel García Márquez has said that he felt blocked as a novelist after writing his first four books and that it was only his life-changing discovery of Pedro Páramo in 1961 that opened his </span>
  <span id="freeText3708862358697431722" style="display:none">“She [your mother] told me you were coming. She said you’d arrive today.”<br>“My mother…my mother is dead.”<br>“Oh, then that’s why her voice sounded so weak.” <br><br>This book, really a novella (120 pages), is a Mexican classic, an early example of magical realism. It’s original, startling, unique. According to Wikipedia Gabriel García Márquez has said that he felt blocked as a novelist after writing his first four books and that it was only his life-changing discovery of Pedro Páramo in 1961 that opened his way to the composition of his masterpiece, One Hundred Years of Solitude. Jorge Luis Borges considered Pedro Páramo to be one of the greatest texts written in any language. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1557229451i/27469170.jpg" width="363" height="242" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>A man’s mother dies and he promised her on her death bed that he would go back to her village to meet his father, Don Pedro, whom he has never met. Near the village he meets a man who tells him Pedro was his father too and that “He’s hate. He’s just pure hate.”<br><br>The god-forsaken village is dead or dying and it’s filled with ghosts. Maybe a few are real people, but most are ghosts. <br><br>“The village is full of echoes. Perhaps they got trapped in the hollow of the walls, or under the stones. When you walk in the street you can hear other footsteps, and rustling noises, and laughter. Old laughter, as if it were tired of laughing by now. And voices worn out with use. You can hear all this. I think someday these sounds will die away.”<br><br>It’s hard to keep track of who is who and who is alive. Maybe they are all dead. Perhaps this is purgatory because there is a lot of talk of the priest, confessions, and waiting for sins to be forgiven. “Can’t you see my sins? Can’t you see those purple stains, like impetigo? And that’s only on the outside. Inside I’m a sea of mud.”<br><br>The noises that the ghosts hear are constant and Stephen-Kingish: screams, animals, men banging on doors with guns.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1557229708i/27469222._SX540_.jpg" width="325" height="325" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>A very short book, well-worth a read and quite a trip. <br><br>Catorce, a Mexican ghost town from dailymail.co.uk <br>Photo of the author (1917-1986) from notimerica.com<br><br><br><br><br><br><br> <br></span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer591092684" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer17338466528180576300"><b>Juan Rulfo</b> was one of those who stood at the beginning of magic realism.<br/><i>Pedro Páramo</i> is a descent into the hell of human memory, a plunge into an abyss of the dire past – the hero travels to find his father but he finds himself astray in the land of the dead.<br/><blockquote>Behind him, as he left, he heard the murmuring.<br/>I am lying in the same bed where my mother died so long ago; on the same mattress, beneath the same black wool coverlet she wrapped us in to sleep. I slept beside her, her little girl, in the sp</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText17338466528180576300" style="display:none"><b>Juan Rulfo</b> was one of those who stood at the beginning of magic realism.<br /><i>Pedro Páramo</i> is a descent into the hell of human memory, a plunge into an abyss of the dire past – the hero travels to find his father but he finds himself astray in the land of the dead.<br /><blockquote>Behind him, as he left, he heard the murmuring.<br />I am lying in the same bed where my mother died so long ago; on the same mattress, beneath the same black wool coverlet she wrapped us in to sleep. I slept beside her, her little girl, in the special place she made for me in her arms.<br />I think I can still feel the calm rhythm of her breathing; the palpitations and sighs that soothed my sleep... I think I feel the pain of her death... But that isn't true.<br />Here I lie, flat on my back, hoping to forget my loneliness by remembering those times.<br />Because I am not here just for a while. And I am not in my mother's bed but in a black box like the ones for burying the dead. Because I am dead.</blockquote><br />The novel is the Gehenna of despair and the Tartarus of sorrow and there is nowhere to hide so one must pass through the labyrinth of insufferable agony.<br />Destiny makes us travel though the strange valleys…</span>
  <a data-text-id="17338466528180576300" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_156503765" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/156503765" />
    <a title="WILLIAM2" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4684009-william2"><img alt="WILLIAM2" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530030828p2/4684009.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/156503765?book_show_action=true">Mar 24, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="WILLIAM2" class="user" itemprop="url" name="WILLIAM2" href="/user/show/4684009-william2">WILLIAM2</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4684009-william2?shelf=mexico">mexico</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4684009-william2?shelf=translation">translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4684009-william2?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4684009-william2?shelf=20-ce">20-ce</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4684009-william2?shelf=latin-america">latin-america</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer156503765" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8688181229483007492">Second reading. Surprisingly readable prose for such a dense and multi-layered story. A young man follows his mother's dying wish to return to the village of her birth and make Pedro Páramo, the young man's father, pay for the abandonment of his family. What follows is something like Dante's descent into hell as the young man, Juan Preciado, and his Virgil, a burro-driver named Abundio — also a son of Páramo — make their way down the long road to the village. The village of the mother's youth is</span>
  <span id="freeText8688181229483007492" style="display:none">Second reading. Surprisingly readable prose for such a dense and multi-layered story. A young man follows his mother's dying wish to return to the village of her birth and make Pedro Páramo, the young man's father, pay for the abandonment of his family. What follows is something like Dante's descent into hell as the young man, Juan Preciado, and his Virgil, a burro-driver named Abundio — also a son of Páramo — make their way down the long road to the village. The village of the mother's youth is now a ghost town in which the living and the dead meet freely. What we might call the present action is rendered in the first-person voice of Juan Preciado. Spasmodically then the prose will switch to a third-person narration of life in the village long ago. The Páramos are a murderous bunch of thieves who take what they want, including the young women, who are always inexplicably grateful for being knocked up by them. Once we've switched to the third-person voice and back a few times, we begin to get a number of other first-person voices from those who once lived in the village. But don't let this put you off, for despite the multiple voices and a few touches of surrealism the book's not at all difficult for those who read attentively. (Susan Sontag introduces the text with a bit of well-earned praise and an explanation of how influential <i>Pedro Páramo</i> has been among Latin-American writers.) I suppose my favorite sequence is when those buried in the local graveyard listen to each other and comment on what is being said! Superficially, the novella seems close to Machado de Assis's own worthwhile <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/87262.The_Posthumous_Memoirs_of_Br_s_Cubas" title="The Posthumous Memoirs of Brás Cubas by Machado de Assis" rel="nofollow noopener"> The Posthumous Memoirs of Bras Cubas</a>, but that's an acerbic comedy compared to this piece of profound gravitas. Not to be missed.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8688181229483007492" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5498525-fionnuala?shelf=translated-from-spanish">translated-from-spanish</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3011365400" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16224972357518781918">People often talk about 'Before and After', as in before something momentous happens and after it has happened. <br/><br/>There's a 'Before and After' in this book, and though the transition between the two happens from one moment to the next, there's an immeasurable distance between them in everything except time. I think of that distance as the distance between the town of Colima and the town of Comala, both real places in Mexico. <br/><br/>When his mother dies, Juan Preciado sets out from his home in Colima to f</span>
  <span id="freeText16224972357518781918" style="display:none">People often talk about 'Before and After', as in before something momentous happens and after it has happened. <br /><br />There's a 'Before and After' in this book, and though the transition between the two happens from one moment to the next, there's an immeasurable distance between them in everything except time. I think of that distance as the distance between the town of Colima and the town of Comala, both real places in Mexico. <br /><br />When his mother dies, Juan Preciado sets out from his home in Colima to find his father, Pedro Páramo, in Comala. As he approaches Comala, he meets a man called Abundio at a crossroads called Los Encuentros. That well-named crossroads marks the transition from Before to After. <br /><br />After, for Juan Preciado, is when 'from one moment to the next' no longer exists, all moments are now concurrent, and all places have disappeared except Comala. There is no more Time and no more Distance. There is only Comala.<br /><br />As I read about Pedro Páramo and Juan Preciado, and Susana San Juan and her Florencio, and Abundio Martinez and his Cuca, and Eduviges and Dolorita, and Damiana and Dorotea, and Father Rentería and Ana, and Miguel Páramo and El Colorado, the words seemed to shift about on the bright white page, replacing and repeating each other, scattering and reforming like birds in the evening until finally disappearing, taking their meaning with them. <br /><br />Then I'd turn the blue-shadowed page and the words would be back in their bright white places, and I'd keep turning pages until once again, the words would fade into the blue shadows and disappear. I became so used to this pattern that when there were no more pages left to turn, I went back to the beginning and started the bright white pages all over again, watching now for the moments when the words would take off like birds at evening, and following along behind them. <br /><br />I'd learned how to read Pedro Páramo.<br /><br />There is a Before and After in my reading life. Before I read Pedro Páramo and after I read Pedro Páramo</span>
  <a data-text-id="16224972357518781918" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_104739170" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/104739170" />
    <a title="Agnieszka" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2446508-agnieszka"><img alt="Agnieszka" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1371835955p2/2446508.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/104739170?book_show_action=true">Sep 01, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Agnieszka" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Agnieszka" href="/user/show/2446508-agnieszka">Agnieszka</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781781253168" href="/book/show/22931012-pedro-p-ramo">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=2019">2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=latin-america">latin-america</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer104739170" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11050779721191860653"><br/><i>This town is filled with echoes. It's like they were trapped behind the walls, or beneath the cobblestones. When you walk you feel like someone's behind you, stepping in your footsteps.</i><br/><br/>Juan Preciado promises his dying mother to travel to her home village Comala to visit his father, the title Pedro Páramo, and claim what’s theirs. This is the starting point of the novel. So he sets off but Comala from his mother’s tales is quite a different thing. Today it is a dead town, where ghosts of the past</span>
  <span id="freeText11050779721191860653" style="display:none"><br /><i>This town is filled with echoes. It's like they were trapped behind the walls, or beneath the cobblestones. When you walk you feel like someone's behind you, stepping in your footsteps.</i><br /><br />Juan Preciado promises his dying mother to travel to her home village Comala to visit his father, the title Pedro Páramo, and claim what’s theirs. This is the starting point of the novel. So he sets off but Comala from his mother’s tales is quite a different thing. Today it is a dead town, where ghosts of the past remind only of defeats and failures. Though it is not a silent place at all. In each house, on every square you may hear whispers and lamenting. Over bygone times, unrequited loves and hardships falling on its inhabitants. At times it reminded me Malcolm Lowry’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18481710.Under_the_Volcano" title="Under the Volcano by Malcolm Lowry" rel="nofollow noopener">Under the Volcano</a> with its chaotic narration and deliric voices. <br /><br />I’ve read quite a number novels from Ibero-American writers but somehow Juan Rulfo did escape my attention earlier. And it was probably him who started all this glorious parade of Latin-American authors. Gabriel Garcia Marquez claimed to know the novel by heart and maintained that his iconic today <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/21032147.One_Hundred_Years_of_Solitude" title="One Hundred Years of Solitude by Gabriel García Márquez" rel="nofollow noopener">One Hundred Years of Solitude</a> was strongly influenced by <i>Pedro Páramo</i>. And I can believe why. But there when GGM or Vargas Llosa or Fuentes are ornate, baroque even Juan Rulfo’s style feels quite restricted, with no trace of almost proverbial floweriness so representative for Latin culture and life.<br /><br />I’m not good at Mexican literaure and culture but I guess it’s still full of such Pedro Páramos, men who because they want to or simply can, reach for lands of their neighbours, their wives and daughters, and if it’s not enough for their lives too. <i>Pedro Páramo</i> ingeniously combines historical facts with local beliefs and mythology. But it’s more than that. The novel is foremost the feeling reader is left with, it’s ambience, it’s desolate, desertic landscape. And stories of people long gone, told in kind of stream of consciousness though at times it’s hard to say who’s talking at the moment. It's nonlinear structure that resembles rather hallucinatory dream than neat narrative.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11050779721191860653" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_295408415" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/295408415" />
    <a title="Fabian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1036893-fabian"><img alt="Fabian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514061293p2/1036893.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/295408415?book_show_action=true">Mar 16, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fabian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fabian" href="/user/show/1036893-fabian">Fabian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer295408415" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1699523206013510745">A complete panorama composed of mood & atmosphere, "Pedro Paramo" came highly recommended by Mario Vargas Llosa in his "Letters to a Young Novelist" aka the writer's own poetics. I must say that I had some difficulty with the Spanish at first; it took me longer to get through the short book than I intended. The different vignettes come together to form the corpus of the awful man, brutal rancher, sadistic ladies man, Pedro Paramo (like different POVs coalesce in Mrs. Dalloway to describe & embod</span>
  <span id="freeText1699523206013510745" style="display:none">A complete panorama composed of mood &amp; atmosphere, "Pedro Paramo" came highly recommended by Mario Vargas Llosa in his "Letters to a Young Novelist" aka the writer's own poetics. I must say that I had some difficulty with the Spanish at first; it took me longer to get through the short book than I intended. The different vignettes come together to form the corpus of the awful man, brutal rancher, sadistic ladies man, Pedro Paramo (like different POVs coalesce in Mrs. Dalloway to describe &amp; embody the actual character). <br /><br />Certainly its an exercise in style. Like Hitchcock's famous "Psycho" shower scene in which the protagonist meets her demise halfway through the story, this one has a voice which talks from beyond the grave. All the different anecdotes, colors, and feelings mesh in the end, &amp; it is assumed that the different ghosts of the tragically-tainted town all have their say. Here's a conglomeration of different voices and stories, of different experiences throughout time, through life &amp; death. Authentic &amp; extraordinarily unique.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1699523206013510745" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_717141465" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/717141465" />
    <a title="Garima" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7915003-garima"><img alt="Garima" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1360053697p2/7915003.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/717141465?book_show_action=true">Sep 11, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Garima" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Garima" href="/user/show/7915003-garima">Garima</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer717141465" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8440112426441371021"><br/><i>. . . I watched the trickles glinting in the lightning flashes, and every breath I breathed, I sighed. And every thought I thought was of you, Susana.</i><br/><br/>Like a message in the bottle, some stories float through decades and centuries on the endless ocean of an untold past and bear a timeless appeal by echoing few words of eternal desires – Wish you were here. <i>Pedro Paramo</i> is one such story. Surrounded by an iridescence of magical realism, the wonders of this gorgeous little book can’t truly be captur</span>
  <span id="freeText8440112426441371021" style="display:none"><br /><i>. . . I watched the trickles glinting in the lightning flashes, and every breath I breathed, I sighed. And every thought I thought was of you, Susana.</i><br /><br />Like a message in the bottle, some stories float through decades and centuries on the endless ocean of an untold past and bear a timeless appeal by echoing few words of eternal desires – Wish you were here. <i>Pedro Paramo</i> is one such story. Surrounded by an iridescence of magical realism, the wonders of this gorgeous little book can’t truly be captured in the few sentences of English language probably because Juan Rulfo penned his only novel in the language of a different world and obliged us a glimpse of that world through his words. <br /><br /><i> The moon had risen briefly and then slipped out of sight. It was one of those sad moons that no one looks at or pays attention to. It had hung there a while, misshapen, not shedding any light, and then gone to hide behind the hills.</i><br /><br />It recounts the tale of a fateful Mexican village where the line of past and present, death and life, hate and love is merged into creating a hazy phantasmagoria that doesn’t demand to be understood but rather immersed in its flow of haunting yet mesmerizing narration. A full circle of loss and search by exploring the dynamics of human memories on hearing the faint sounds of phantom voices is impeccably drawn by Juan Rulfo through his understated and lucid prose. Highly recommended as a perfect reading companion for a short and memorable meeting.<br /></span>

<div id="review_856191036" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/856191036" />
    <a title="Stephen P" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11602286-stephen-p"><img alt="Stephen P" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1461732018p2/11602286.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/856191036?book_show_action=true">Feb 16, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephen P" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephen P" href="/user/show/11602286-stephen-p">Stephen P</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11602286-stephen-p?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11602286-stephen-p?shelf=re-read">re-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11602286-stephen-p?shelf=latin-american">latin-american</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer856191036" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10257364380727439752"><br/><br/>								<br/><br/>Spoken as a literary dream this grim tale bordering the fine line of fable switches past and present, points of view, with whispered elegance. Images are presented out of swirls of dust and cloud myth, tale and hallucination, revealing the cutthroat lives of existence.<br/><br/>I just finished Knausgaard's, My Struggle #1 and #2 wouldn't arrive for two days. Unable to be without reading a book I picked the slimmest off my shelves in my library. Even being a slow reader 124 pages could be finished </span>
  <span id="freeText10257364380727439752" style="display:none"><br /><br />								<br /><br />Spoken as a literary dream this grim tale bordering the fine line of fable switches past and present, points of view, with whispered elegance. Images are presented out of swirls of dust and cloud myth, tale and hallucination, revealing the cutthroat lives of existence.<br /><br />I just finished Knausgaard's, My Struggle #1 and #2 wouldn't arrive for two days. Unable to be without reading a book I picked the slimmest off my shelves in my library. Even being a slow reader 124 pages could be finished in two days. It opened an apocalyptic future vision of books being sold by the pound in a butcher shop. <br /><br />Beginning to read I was shocked by what I had in my hands. Please think of this as a provisional review having slipped from the nest prematurely. I wish I had Rulpho's artistic genius to pare and mold this review into what it need to be. Lacking such I will re-read this maybe a couple of times, adding to or subtracting from this review while asking for your patience.<br /><br />Juan Preciado is asked by his dying mother to find a man named Pedro Parama in Comala and receive the money owed them for his disinteredness and their need to abandon him in the past. Pedro has been dead for some time. The town of Comala appears abandoned, desecrated, stuck in dust and decay. Juan finds it cared for spiritually by a lapsed priest and the legends of the amoral ruling Paramo family. The citizenry are souls, those already dead but looking for someone alive who has not sinned or one who has been forgiven and can pray for them, their release. The priest does not meet these parameters, neither do the flitting shadows still remaining. There is no one to lift these souls further. The line between the living and dead has been calcified. Juan has found history, myth, hallucinations, and the unsettled bone dust of death. He has stumbled upon life on earth, a living death of sinners where there are no forgiven left to pray for the wandering souls. We live a life of limbo.<br /><br />Rulfo worked on this, his only novel, over some time having written much then lifted an angel's scythe and lessened it to its essence. This story seems to tell itself. It conveys what it wishes to say without any apparent intrusion by Rulfo. Earning his wages through non-literary means, he sold tires, had a family, wrote and listened to music at night, writing his grand novel. As Michelangelo, he spent much time paring down the white marble to find the essence of the art within. The natural flow of the spare beauty of his words make the unnatural natural, and sets a tone and mood that places our readerly mind within the literature of dream yet laden with meaning.<br /><br />Paring down must be one of the more difficult pursuits of an author. Yet when years later Rulfo placed his scalpel onto the tabletop and looked before him, tears must have flooded his eyes. This is a book that lives beyond the limits of category, precursor, movements spawned. It belongs to the few books, and please do not shoot the messenger, that when you die, and you will, you will want to know this is a book you read and experienced. This will not be due to fame, popularity, but because it speaks of the swirled dust midair and all that cannot be said. Please read this book for your own soul.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10257364380727439752" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_138987387" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/138987387" />
    <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely"><img alt="K.D. Absolutely" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471967140p2/2156200.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/138987387?book_show_action=true">Jan 06, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="K.D. Absolutely" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely">K.D. Absolutely</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to K.D. by: </span>
      1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die (2006-2010)
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=latin-american">latin-american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=1001-core">1001-core</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer138987387" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11333989051919782896">Absolutely brilliant. First published in 1955, <i>Pedro Paramo</i> was the only novel by Mexican author and photographer, <b>Juan Rulfo</b>, yet it established his name as one of the most important Spanish-language writers of the 20th century together with <i>Jorge Luis Borges</i>. This novel started the genre, <i>magical realism</i> that inspired Gabriel Garcia Marquez to write his masterpiece, <i>100 Years of Solitude</i>. In fact, Marquez liked the novel so much that he read it many times and could recite portions of it for ma</span>
  <span id="freeText11333989051919782896" style="display:none">Absolutely brilliant. First published in 1955, <i>Pedro Paramo</i> was the only novel by Mexican author and photographer, <b>Juan Rulfo</b>, yet it established his name as one of the most important Spanish-language writers of the 20th century together with <i>Jorge Luis Borges</i>. This novel started the genre, <i>magical realism</i> that inspired Gabriel Garcia Marquez to write his masterpiece, <i>100 Years of Solitude</i>. In fact, Marquez liked the novel so much that he read it many times and could recite portions of it for many years. Once again, this book proved that quality weighs more than quantity when it comes to making a mark in literary world. <br /><br />From the first sentence of the book, <i>"I came to Comala because I had been told that my father, a man named Pedro Paramo, lived there."</i> up to its last sentence, I was in awe. It is a well-crafted story. The narration is predominantly first person but in multiple points of view. The time reference is shifting between present and past. The magic realism is basically on the fact that the people in Comala are already dead and when the narrator, i.e., the son of Pedro Paramo, by the name of <b>Juan Preciado</b> arrives in Comala, he has to die in order to know the story behind his mother, his father and all the ghosts of Comala which seems like a purgatory.<br /><br />What this book wants to impart its readers is that life and death are not in difference phases. Rather, death is a part of life or is death is the final stage of being alive. In the book, the ghosts are like human beings: they have feelings, dreams, everyday problems, etc. Rulfo seems to be saying that the life after "death" is like a second life if not a continuation of life, just like when you wake up in another country during your business trip or when you migrate in another country without any plan of going back to your country of origin.<br /><br />Which for me is where the beauty of the story is. Of course, Rulfo made this believable by throwing in his savvy in story-telling: complex yet effective, multi-layered yet unified, shifting yet easy to follow. I wonder why he chose only to have 1 novel during his lifetime.<br /><br />One of the books that I intend to re-read someday.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11333989051919782896" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2518516510" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14488283170394249873"><i>Pedro Páramo</i> is filled with beauty and sadness. Told in fragments, the novel constantly shifts perspectives, blending past and future, living and dead, in chaotic, unpredictable ways. This makes the narrative challenging to follow, but creates a chilling, dreamlike atmosphere, and a kind of extra-temporal unification of cause and effect. There is an implied tragedy at the heart of the novel, the nature of which is gradually revealed, though never completely.<br/><br/>Because of its disconnected and crypti</span>
  <span id="freeText14488283170394249873" style="display:none"><i>Pedro Páramo</i> is filled with beauty and sadness. Told in fragments, the novel constantly shifts perspectives, blending past and future, living and dead, in chaotic, unpredictable ways. This makes the narrative challenging to follow, but creates a chilling, dreamlike atmosphere, and a kind of extra-temporal unification of cause and effect. There is an implied tragedy at the heart of the novel, the nature of which is gradually revealed, though never completely.<br /><br />Because of its disconnected and cryptic structure, and perhaps also my own cultural distance, I felt quite detached from the narrative. This novel would benefit from multiple readings, though I suspect that even with close study it will never fully relinquish its mystery and ambiguity.</span>

<div id="review_805848489" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/805848489" />
    <a title="Brian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1938805-brian"><img alt="Brian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442608867p2/1938805.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/805848489?book_show_action=true">Dec 30, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brian" href="/user/show/1938805-brian">Brian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Brian by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/4193758-mike-puma">Mike Puma</a>
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer805848489" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3673251662198140637">A Mexican magical realism's response to <i>The Spoon River Anthology</i>, Rulfo's brilliant novella isn't going to give up its goods on the first reading. Beyond the requirement of careful character and dialogue parsing, I sense there are different ways to approach the text. We learn in the opening sentences that our protagonist is on his way to visit a father he has never met at the behest of a death-bed request from his mother. After reading this book twice now (and the reviews of my brilliant GR fri</span>
  <span id="freeText3673251662198140637" style="display:none">A Mexican magical realism's response to <i>The Spoon River Anthology</i>, Rulfo's brilliant novella isn't going to give up its goods on the first reading. Beyond the requirement of careful character and dialogue parsing, I sense there are different ways to approach the text. We learn in the opening sentences that our protagonist is on his way to visit a father he has never met at the behest of a death-bed request from his mother. After reading this book twice now (and the reviews of my brilliant GR friends), <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I'm not entirely certain that the narrator isn't dead, too. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />If this book isn't a forefather of <i>One Hundred Years of Solitude</i>, it is definitely a godparent. Reading this book today made me miss Marquez's brilliant work and reminded me of all his others I want to read.<br /><br />A fabulous read. My only complaint is that it didn't go on for another 300 pages.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3673251662198140637" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_178959424" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/178959424" />
    <a title="Ben Winch" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5647943-ben-winch"><img alt="Ben Winch" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605154548p2/5647943.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/178959424?book_show_action=true">Jun 22, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ben Winch" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ben Winch" href="/user/show/5647943-ben-winch">Ben Winch</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5647943-ben-winch?shelf=5-stars">5-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5647943-ben-winch?shelf=mexican">mexican</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5647943-ben-winch?shelf=latin-american">latin-american</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer178959424" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10430856500908712613"><b>Attempt 2, 2014:</b><br/><br/>It’s hard to describe what this book means to me. On about my second reading I wrote the kneejerk emotive “You Should Read This” response below. Now, on my fourth or fifth, I wonder if I’m any closer to a lucid appraisal.<br/><br/>He doesn’t give you much, Rulfo. If there’s such thing as a minimalist he’s probably it, though not with the magnifying-glass focus of late-period Beckett, or the plainspokenness of Hemingway or Carver. Gothic, otherworldly, broad (though not vast) in scope, <i>Pedr</i></span>
  <span id="freeText10430856500908712613" style="display:none"><b>Attempt 2, 2014:</b><br /><br />It’s hard to describe what this book means to me. On about my second reading I wrote the kneejerk emotive “You Should Read This” response below. Now, on my fourth or fifth, I wonder if I’m any closer to a lucid appraisal.<br /><br />He doesn’t give you much, Rulfo. If there’s such thing as a minimalist he’s probably it, though not with the magnifying-glass focus of late-period Beckett, or the plainspokenness of Hemingway or Carver. Gothic, otherworldly, broad (though not vast) in scope, <i>Pedro Paramo</i> is a book about haunting that haunts you, a collection of silences that stuns you to silence, a Brechtian jangle of half-heard monologues that echoes once it’s over, the printed words triggers for the whispers that endure. If <i>Pedro Paramo</i> is sparsely told, it’s only because Rulfo wants to remain invisible – wants his characters’ voices to reach us undiminished. Of all the 20 or 30 writers central to my love of literature, Rulfo may be most instinctive. <i>Pedro Paramo</i> proceeded, so it’s said, by a process of elimination. Rulfo’s first book, <i>The Burning Plain</i>, was a series of stories more earthy, less focussed, rawer, less “literary”. This too could have gone in that direction: it <i>is</i> a series of stories, maybe was originally written as such, then shuffled and combined with only a sense of beauty as guide. By about halfway, every time, I shiver; I look with new focus. Hasn’t the entire book just been inverted? And truth to tell, there may be something just the tiniest bit Hollywood in it. But it’s great! It could be filmed now (is being filmed, I think, starring Gael Garcia Bernal) and would not seem like a historical piece. It’s Hammett’s <i>Nightmare Town</i> meets Marquez’s <i>Leaf Storm</i>. It’s <i>The Spoon River Anthology</i> told by ghosts set in Mexico. It’s everything that’s great about noir and myth and tragedy pressure-cooked and exploded across the expressionist/humanist tapestry of <i>The Burning Plain</i>. Written before its author was 40, it’s just possible it haunted him for the rest of his life, during which 30 more years he exhibited photos, worked at the National Institute for Indigenous People in Mexico and published one novella, unavailable in English, yet is remembered till this day as possibly Mexico’s greatest modern writer. <br /><br /><b>Attempt 1, 2012:</b><br /><br />A household name in Mexico, and acknowledged in the Spanish-speaking world as one of the pre-eminent Latin American authors, Juan Rulfo published only two slim volumes in his lifetime: <i>Pedro Paramo</i>, a novel, and <i>The Burning Plain</i>, a collection of short stories. Maybe this is why he is so little known in the Anglo world. Whatever the reason, it’s oversights like this that rob our Anglo-centric literature of the key insights that could transform it. That Rulfo was central in creating what became “magical realism” should be understood by anyone who embraces that hackneyed marketing term. Yet <i>Pedro Paramo</i> cannot be encapsulated by that term. One of the most forcefully unique, instinctual, gritty novels I have read, this is a true classic, which should be studied in schools around the world, not just in Latin America.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10430856500908712613" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_859957928" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/859957928" />
    <a title="Kenny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1642452-kenny"><img alt="Kenny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472073986p2/1642452.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/859957928?book_show_action=true">Feb 19, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kenny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kenny" href="/user/show/1642452-kenny">Kenny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1642452-kenny?shelf=spenky-says-so">spenky-says-so</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1642452-kenny?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1642452-kenny?shelf=magical-realism">magical-realism</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer859957928" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9148695618731495164"><b><i>“The sky was filled with fat stars, swollen from the long night. The moon had risen briefly and then slipped out of sight. It was one of those sad moons that no one looks at or pays attention to. It had hung there a while, misshapen, not shedding any light, and then gone to hide behind the hills.”</i> <br/>Juan Rulfo ~~ Pedro Páramo</b><br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1397178694i/9243162.jpg' alt='one"/' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>There are few books that leave me speechless when turning the lasting page. Pedro Paramo was one such book. Pedro Paramo may also be the scariest ghost story ever written.<br/><br/>Auth</span>
  <span id="freeText9148695618731495164" style="display:none"><b><i>“The sky was filled with fat stars, swollen from the long night. The moon had risen briefly and then slipped out of sight. It was one of those sad moons that no one looks at or pays attention to. It had hung there a while, misshapen, not shedding any light, and then gone to hide behind the hills.”</i> <br>Juan Rulfo ~~ Pedro Páramo</b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1397178694i/9243162.jpg" alt='one"/' class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>There are few books that leave me speechless when turning the lasting page. Pedro Paramo was one such book. Pedro Paramo may also be the scariest ghost story ever written.<br><br>Author Juan Rulfo's extraordinarily powerful novel, Pedro Paramo, captures the crux of life in rural Mexico during the last years of the 19th century, and the beginning of the 20th, like no other work of fiction. Here, the author vividly portrays the radical social and economic changes which spurred the dramatic migration from ranches and villages to the urban slums, where they could no longer live off the land, nor find work. Ghost towns mark the places where many had once flourished. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1397178694i/9243163.jpg" alt='two"/' class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Pedro Paramo, the son of a failing landowner, was consumed with love for Susana San Juan. This intense passion lasted a lifetime. Eventually, Pedro's aging father and family died, and Susana moved away. Alone and lonely, he assumed control of the estate and unscrupulously did whatever he had to, fair and foul, to amass a fortune and build his empire. He married the heiress Dolores Preciado, took possession of her land and wealth, and sent her to live an isolated existence with her sister. His ranch, in Comala, the Media Luna, expanded with great success at the expense of others. However, the manipulative, exploitive patriarch would pay dearly for his greed and for the sorrow he brought to Comala and her people. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1397178772i/9243167.jpg" alt='three"/' class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Dolores Preciado, on her deathbed, extracts a promise from her son, Juan, to return to Comala to find his father and claim what is theirs. Juan narrates and guides the reader on his journey to the dusty, desolate village, now populated by ghosts, lost souls who murmur to him, sighing and complaining in desperate voices, until he believes that he too is dead. The story of Juan's experience, his search for identity and his heritage, is interwoven with the tale of his father, Pedro Paramo, and that of sad, beautiful Susana San Juan. <br><br>The novel was first published in 1955 and has become a classic, not only in Spanish speaking countries, but worldwide, for its themes are universal. This is a literary class and a truly great book. I cannot recommend it highly enough.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9148695618731495164" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_558305188" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/558305188" />
    <a title="Ema" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5746897-ema"><img alt="Ema" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1394635581p2/5746897.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/558305188?book_show_action=true">Mar 11, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ema" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ema" href="/user/show/5746897-ema">Ema</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788433920706" href="/book/show/38664.Pedro_P_ramo">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5746897-ema?shelf=mexico">mexico</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5746897-ema?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5746897-ema?shelf=bookish-gems">bookish-gems</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5746897-ema?shelf=read-in-2013">read-in-2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5746897-ema?shelf=read-in-spanish">read-in-spanish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5746897-ema?shelf=magical-and-surreal">magical-and-surreal</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer558305188" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2893793880557554933">At the end of a lot of struggling days and a 70-pages document with new words, I've managed to finish my first novel read in Spanish. As I feel like I've earned a prize, I have to thank Linda and Dolors for their support!<br/> <br/>I'm not sure I've chosen the right novel to begin with. Apart from my poor understanding of language, the plot was bringing more confusion than I could deal with. I felt utterly frustrated at times because I wanted to read quicker and understand what was going on. But then, the</span>
  <span id="freeText2893793880557554933" style="display:none">At the end of a lot of struggling days and a 70-pages document with new words, I've managed to finish my first novel read in Spanish. As I feel like I've earned a prize, I have to thank Linda and Dolors for their support!<br /> <br />I'm not sure I've chosen the right novel to begin with. Apart from my poor understanding of language, the plot was bringing more confusion than I could deal with. I felt utterly frustrated at times because I wanted to read quicker and understand what was going on. But then, the slow pace made me taste mouthfuls of the wonderful sound of Spanish. Man oh man, this language is musicality itself! In the right hands, it breaths poetry through every word. And Juan Rulfo has the magic hands, this can't be denied.<br /><br />Because of my slow and fragmented foray into this novel, I tend to think of it as a dream or a hallucinatory sequence, much like one from Twin Peaks, where flashes of light reveal and obscure situations and people. Or maybe it's just how it truly feels, maybe I've got the right taste of it. Reality, or what we believe is reality, shifts unexpectedly to a different place and time, transforming the novel into a giant Rubik's Cube with all the colors mixed up. José Donoso's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/382975.The_Obscene_Bird_of_Night" title="The Obscene Bird of Night by José Donoso" rel="nofollow noopener">The Obscene Bird of Night</a> comes to mind now, although this one was pure madness of tangled situations and personalities.<br /><br />Juan Preciado comes to Comala to find his father, but he is met by an apparently deserted village, hotter than hell, so different from the luscious place depicted in his mother's memories. He can hear murmurs and talk, but no people are in sight. Gradually, through eerie circumstances, he meets some of the residents of Comala, old women and men behaving strangely. This thread of narration is interrupted by echoes from another world - we hear thoughts of a different character, concealed and mysterious at first, on a lush background of rain and greenery; we come to know scattered events that happened long ago, in the time of Pedro Páramo. The people that Juan meets inhabit both the present and the past, resembling the pieces of a puzzle that fall slowly into place. <br /><br />I was spellbound by the story of Juan Preciado and his journey through Comala; I love magical realism and this part of the novel went straight to my heart. Five stars without hesitation. The level of enjoyment was not the same for the story of Pedro Páramo, because I had so much to hate about his character. But what the heck, this novel deserves five stars, after all. It was magical, enthralling, obsessive, hateful, all into one. It made a part of Mexico come alive - this country fascinates me, by the way - with the good and the bad and with a heavy dose of surreal. <br /><br />The novel is saturated with the Mexican obsession of death, which I find quite fascinating. If I'll ever go to Mexico, I'll make sure to be there on Día de Muertos, spending my night in the cemetery alight with candles, along with the Mexicans. Ending on a black humor note, I just hope I'll be above ground and not below, like <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Juan Preciado <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. (don't click it unless you've read the novel!!)</span>
  <a data-text-id="2893793880557554933" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_869431836" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/869431836" />
    <a title="Mala" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5960716-mala"><img alt="Mala" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1435586901p2/5960716.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/869431836?book_show_action=true">Mar 02, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mala" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mala" href="/user/show/5960716-mala">Mala</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Nathan NR Gaddis,Zadignose</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Mala by: </span>
      Brain Pain Group
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5960716-mala?shelf=all-time-favourites">all-time-favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5960716-mala?shelf=dream-fantasy-horror">dream-fantasy-horror</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer869431836" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer242669465722770470"><br/>Where the living and the dead worlds collide!<br/>To me this is the perfect bedtime adult ghost story and also the one to be told around a campfire with the appropriate sound effects of howling winds,rustling leaves,and of course the murmurs,always the murmurings escaping from the earth below and through the cracks in the surrounding trees,and a full moon and the"fat stars" and then the rains would start and the said story would be continued inside a tent with a torchlight under the narrator's face f</span>
  <span id="freeText242669465722770470" style="display:none"><br />Where the living and the dead worlds collide!<br />To me this is the perfect bedtime adult ghost story and also the one to be told around a campfire with the appropriate sound effects of howling winds,rustling leaves,and of course the murmurs,always the murmurings escaping from the earth below and through the cracks in the surrounding trees,and a full moon and the"fat stars" and then the rains would start and the said story would be continued inside a tent with a torchlight under the narrator's face for spooky effect. Some people might get a heart attack!<br /><br />Octavio Paz has said that Juan Rulfo "is the only Mexican novelist who has given us an image—instead of just a description—of our landscape."<br />And that's cause this book is all about *atmosphere* and *subtlety* and its small size perfectly suits that purpose for it would've been impossible to sustain that level of narrative tension for a hundred pages more. Which reminds me of another atmospheric ghost story- <i>The Turn of the Screw</i>,but whereas Henry James' book is driven mainly by psychological tension and suspence,this one's saturated surrealism leaves no doubt about its ghostly inhabitants- Rulfo treads a fine line here- in the hands of a lesser writer;this material could easily have turned into a sensational gothic horror or worse, maudlin sentimentality.<br /><br />But is this really the book that "started" magical realism?<br />I'm afraid Steven Moore wouldn't agree with that– magical realism has always been part of fiction- The Norse Sagas,The Golden Ass,The Thousand and One Nights- there are so many examples but we may say that Rulfo's book inspired the Latin American brand of magical realism where the political history was so violent that writers could tiptoe around it using this trick- Marquez,and Fuentes' fictions are prime examples of it.<br /><br />Pedro Páramo is a dense book that demands a repeat reading.This book would make a perfect Halloween gift.<br />Highly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="242669465722770470" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
