<div id="review_565579796" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/565579796" />
    <a title="B. P. Rinehart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4635205-b-p-rinehart"><img alt="B. P. Rinehart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1347740735p2/4635205.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/565579796?book_show_action=true">Mar 19, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="B. P. Rinehart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="B. P. Rinehart" href="/user/show/4635205-b-p-rinehart">B. P. Rinehart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780394174464" href="/book/show/1100091.Pedro_P_ramo">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to B. P. by: </span>
      Eligers Elezi
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4635205-b-p-rinehart?shelf=modernism-and-post-modernism-stuff">modernism-and-post-modernism-stuff</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer565579796" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2907237871648842814">"<i>The earth, 'this vale of tears.'</i>" <br/><br/>I first heard of this book in March of 2013. That year was a landmark year for me as a reader. I read some books that year that have stayed with me to this day and changed me from someone who liked books into a bibliophile. I'd read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4934.The_Brothers_Karamazov' title='The Brothers Karamazov by Fyodor Dostoyevsky' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Brothers Karamazov</a> in February of that year and it had broken me. I thought of not reading books again, but stumbled upon comic books which built my appetite and resolve for reading again. But though I decided to read again I wa</span>
  <span id="freeText2907237871648842814" style="display:none">"<i>The earth, 'this vale of tears.'</i>" <br /><br />I first heard of this book in March of 2013. That year was a landmark year for me as a reader. I read some books that year that have stayed with me to this day and changed me from someone who liked books into a bibliophile. I'd read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4934.The_Brothers_Karamazov" title="The Brothers Karamazov by Fyodor Dostoyevsky" rel="nofollow noopener">The Brothers Karamazov</a> in February of that year and it had broken me. I thought of not reading books again, but stumbled upon comic books which built my appetite and resolve for reading again. But though I decided to read again I was still reading at a pace I had not done before (or since). It seemed the spiritual and mental duress that Dostoevsky had put on me caused a bout of biblio-insanity and I read more books that year (not including trade-paperbacks, but still including graphic novels) than I have ever done since. March saw me read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/134018.The_Complete_Poetry_and_Selected_Prose" title="The Complete Poetry and Selected Prose by John Donne" rel="nofollow noopener">The Complete Poetry and Selected Prose</a> of John Donne, Plato's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/597788.The_Trial_and_Death_of_Socrates__Euthyphro__Apology__Crito__Phaedo_" title="The Trial and Death of Socrates (Euthyphro, Apology, Crito, Phaedo) by Plato" rel="nofollow noopener">The Trial and Death of Socrates</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/52037.The_Sickness_unto_Death" title="The Sickness unto Death by Søren Kierkegaard" rel="nofollow noopener">The Sickness unto Death</a> by Kierkegaard and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/83321.Irrational_Man_A_Study_in_Existential_Philosophy" title="Irrational Man A Study in Existential Philosophy by William Barrett" rel="nofollow noopener">Irrational Man: A Study in Existential Philosophy</a> (which I finished the day <u>Pedro Páramo</u> came to my attention). <br /><br />With all these ideas and concepts of what it means to exist, practice philosophy and be holy, some guy named Eligers Elezi sends me a message which basically pleads with me to read a book called <u>Pedro Páramo</u>. I can't share his exact words because he is no longer on this website and my message from him does not exist anymore. But I'll never forget the passionate intensity in which he argued for me reading the book and wanting to see my review of it (I don't like to brag, but I really was on fire that year). Unfortunately, I had a full slate and I was not in the right frame of mind to be persuaded by strangers. I did add the book (and Elezi), but I returned back to my insanity. I'd not read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/13450.Gabriel_Garc_a_M_rquez" title="Gabriel García Márquez" rel="nofollow noopener">Gabriel García Márquez</a> or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3534.Toni_Morrison" title="Toni Morrison" rel="nofollow noopener">Toni Morrison</a> yet--had not even read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/373755.Absalom__Absalom_" title="Absalom, Absalom! by William Faulkner" rel="nofollow noopener">Absalom, Absalom!</a>. Fast-forward to June of 2018 and things have changed.<br /><br /><i>"The village is full of echoes. Perhaps they got trapped in the hollows of the walls, or under stones."</i><br /><br />This book was written post-Faulkner, but pre-Gabo and it is proudly Mexican in its feeling. The use of multiple narratives and settings bleeding into each other is essentially modernist, but the revelation of the town of Comala's present-day residence is magical realism. The blend of these two genes so seamlessly was the big treat of this book to me. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I was also amazed and amused at the idea that ghosts could be haunted by other ghosts and that we could see the thoughts and point-of-views of corpses separate from their spirits. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> I was impressed that this book could keep me so engaged with the narrative--a feat that is owed to Mr. Rulfo and the translator <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/884338.Lysander_Kemp" title="Lysander Kemp" rel="nofollow noopener">Lysander Kemp</a>. The best novels, in my opinion, are both engaging and to-the-point.<br /><br />To wrap this up, it is very obvious where <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12084166.One_Hundred_Years_of_Solitude" title="One Hundred Years of Solitude by Gabriel García Márquez" rel="nofollow noopener">One Hundred Years of Solitude</a> got its inspiration from and I was reminded a lot of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6149.Beloved" title="Beloved by Toni Morrison" rel="nofollow noopener">Beloved</a> while reading this book (though <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11334.Song_of_Solomon" title="Song of Solomon by Toni Morrison" rel="nofollow noopener">Song of Solomon</a> would be a much more plot-relevant comparison). While this book was more concise and straight-forward than <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12084166.One_Hundred_Years_of_Solitude" title="One Hundred Years of Solitude by Gabriel García Márquez" rel="nofollow noopener">One Hundred Years of Solitude</a>, I still prefer the world of Macondo to Comala--though I recognize the former could not exist without the latter<br /><br />"<i>He struck a feeble blow against the ground and then crumbled to pieces as if he were a heap of stones.</i>"</span>
  <a data-text-id="2907237871648842814" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_129345897" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Mike Puma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4193758-mike-puma"><img alt="Mike Puma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1434667514p2/4193758.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/129345897?book_show_action=true">Nov 04, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike Puma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike Puma" href="/user/show/4193758-mike-puma">Mike Puma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">almost anyone </span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Mike by: </span>
      Roberto Bolaño
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4193758-mike-puma?shelf=2011">2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4193758-mike-puma?shelf=mexican-author">mexican-author</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer129345897" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17415288583816655490"><p>Enough is written elsewhere to give you a good sense of the novella. There’s equally enough written elsewhere which speaks to this book’s foundational status with regard to magical realism. If asked, I’d only say <i>Pedro Páramo</i> is <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/205476.Our_Town' title='Our Town by Thornton Wilder' rel='nofollow noopener'>Our Town</a> as conceived by Rod Serling and written by William Faulkner. Helpful? </p><p>It’s great to be able to say this book was recommended to me by Roberto Bolaño—partly because it’s true, partly because it’s exciting to have a great author make recommendations that speak to </p></span>
  <span id="freeText17415288583816655490" style="display:none"><p>Enough is written elsewhere to give you a good sense of the novella. There’s equally enough written elsewhere which speaks to this book’s foundational status with regard to magical realism. If asked, I’d only say <i>Pedro Páramo</i> is <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/205476.Our_Town" title="Our Town by Thornton Wilder" rel="nofollow noopener">Our Town</a> as conceived by Rod Serling and written by William Faulkner. Helpful? </p><p>It’s great to be able to say this book was recommended to me by Roberto Bolaño—partly because it’s true, partly because it’s exciting to have a great author make recommendations that speak to you, and partly because of how it feels to tread the same (reading) path that Bolaño tread. I read this following a brief discussion of the author in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6593980.Roberto_Bola_o_The_Last_Interview___Other_Conversations" title="Roberto Bolaño The Last Interview &amp; Other Conversations by Roberto Bolaño" rel="nofollow noopener">Roberto Bolaño: The Last Interview &amp; Other Conversations</a>, and it’s a wonderfully brief diversion from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/45974.The_Book_of_Disquiet__Penguin_Classics_" title="The Book of Disquiet (Penguin Classics) by Fernando Pessoa" rel="nofollow noopener">The Book of Disquiet</a> to which I can now wholeheartedly return.</p></span>

          </span>

<div id="review_2509017453" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2509017453" />
    <a title="Steven Godin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin"><img alt="Steven Godin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605181037p2/26706841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2509017453?book_show_action=true">Aug 27, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Godin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Godin" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin">Steven Godin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=latin-america">latin-america</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2509017453" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12201065412500954698">Written by immigration agent Juan Rulfo with state funding and published in Mexico in 1955, this psychotic novel does everything one could never dream of if limited by contemporary creative-writing dogma. The book's structure fragments and its protagonist fades out of the narrative, there is no clear plot-line, no hooks, no character development arcs, no climax, no epilogue, and one is left with an existential sense of dislocation and uncertainty. If this novel were to have been written today, w</span>
  <span id="freeText12201065412500954698" style="display:none">Written by immigration agent Juan Rulfo with state funding and published in Mexico in 1955, this psychotic novel does everything one could never dream of if limited by contemporary creative-writing dogma. The book's structure fragments and its protagonist fades out of the narrative, there is no clear plot-line, no hooks, no character development arcs, no climax, no epilogue, and one is left with an existential sense of dislocation and uncertainty. If this novel were to have been written today, what chance that any publisher would have gone near it? very little I would say. Yet the world owes Rulfo a debt of gratitude as without Pedro Páramo 'One Hundred Years of Solitude', 'Hopscotch' or 'Midnight's Children' Wouldn't have existed. Simply the literature of the past 50 years or so, our conception of the relationship between the word and reality, would have been measurably poorer. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12201065412500954698" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Vaso" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15818014-vaso"><img alt="Vaso" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1395921377p2/15818014.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vaso" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vaso" href="/user/show/15818014-vaso">Vaso</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer15278780549050776060">Looking for a father you never met, turns out to be depressing.<br/>While reading this book, I thought I just started a journey, I fell asleep and woke up in to a world full of dreams, in which dead people discuss with alive ones.<br/>The prose is exceptional and you just want it to never end.<br/>In this atmospheric boo, where does life collide with death?<br/>That being said, I recommend this to anyone that wants to read a boo that have inflluenced authors like Gabriel Garcia Markes.<br/><br/></span>

<div id="review_2942010580" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2942010580" />
    <a title="Ken" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/730754-ken"><img alt="Ken" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565570004p2/730754.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2942010580?book_show_action=true">Mar 19, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ken" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ken" href="/user/show/730754-ken">Ken</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/730754-ken?shelf=world-literature">world-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/730754-ken?shelf=finished-in-2020">finished-in-2020</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2942010580" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer910146459151927606">Juan Rulfo---previously "Who?" <br/><br/>It all started when I read a book about reading, one where the author, a short story writer himself, recommended this book (and that) of short stories. One of them was <i>The Burning Plain and Other Stories</i> by an early 20th-century Mexican author, Juan Rulfo.<br/><br/>"Hmn," I said (because I say that a lot), "my World Literature reading could always use a booster shot. I'll buy that book and read it."<br/><br/>Like retiring early, easier said than done. <i>The Burning Plain</i> is out of print</span>
  <span id="freeText910146459151927606" style="display:none">Juan Rulfo---previously "Who?" <br /><br />It all started when I read a book about reading, one where the author, a short story writer himself, recommended this book (and that) of short stories. One of them was <i>The Burning Plain and Other Stories</i> by an early 20th-century Mexican author, Juan Rulfo.<br /><br />"Hmn," I said (because I say that a lot), "my World Literature reading could always use a booster shot. I'll buy that book and read it."<br /><br />Like retiring early, easier said than done. <i>The Burning Plain</i> is out of print, hard to find, not something you're likely to trip upon at an Annie's Book Shop (if these stores exist in many places... damned if I know).<br /><br />Suddenly another book, that still WAS in print, <i>Pedro Páramo</i> came up on the radar. A novel written two years after the stories. So the <i>Burning Plain</i> idea was doused. I bought <i>PP</i>.<br /><br />Starting the book, I ran into some lovely descriptions. I said, "Hey, this guy can write!" and felt I was onto something.<br /><br />But then more characters came in. And the narrative kept jumping from one to the other. And I grew confused. Who? What? When? (Only in Spanish, of course.)<br /><br />I backed up a bit to read the introduction. Uh-oh. Magical realism. Nobody warned me about magical realism. That literary movement and I had a run-in decades ago when I marched into Gabriel Remarkable Marquez's <i>Hundred Years of Solitude</i>. Marched in and marched out. (I believe military historians call it a "retreat.")<br /><br />And so it was here. History repeating itself. The problem in this case? Characters in <i>Pedro Páramo</i> are dead people. They act alive like you and me, but they're dead. Only the narrator is not. At least I don't think he is. Or at least he doesn't seem to be when he's there (which he often isn't, damn him).<br /><br />So it came to this: a slim little 124-page book and I'm slogging through it. Those words don't typically go together---"124-pages" and "slogging through it." Sheesh. Practically a (longish) short story. Or novella, if you will. And a Mexican writer of some renown! And me, philistine, not appreciating it, just because the characters are deader than I am.<br /><br />At least toward the end I hit more pretty writing. Like so: "It was a long time till dawn. The sky was filled with fat stars, swollen from the long night. The moon had risen briefly and then slipped out of sight. It was one of those sad moons that no one looks at or pays attention to. It had hung there a while, mishapen, not shedding any light, and then gone to hide behind the hills."<br /><br />I felt like that moon, slipped out of the book's sight, sad, someone no one (least of all the book, understandably miffed) pays attention to. This as the narrative winds down and people who are already dead begin to die, one by one, just like a Shakespeare tragedy, hey-nonny-nonny.<br /><br />Overall, not as pleasant an experience as I would wish. But it's happened before and it will happen again.<br /><br />And yes, I will continue to read books about reading where authors give me recommendations, magical or not.</span>
  <a data-text-id="910146459151927606" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/26274792" />
    <a title="Madhuri" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/875812-madhuri"><img alt="Madhuri" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1449468810p2/875812.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/26274792?book_show_action=true">Jul 04, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Madhuri" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Madhuri" href="/user/show/875812-madhuri">Madhuri</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer14864357374991240823">I keep trying to write something about it - because it was such a beautiful read. But it is hard to say anything about something so poetic. I loved the book - it takes you to such a different world, and through a web of living memories. </span>

<div id="review_1298903565" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1298903565" />
    <a title="[P]" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/40516522-p"><img alt="[P]" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1486240560p2/40516522.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1298903565?book_show_action=true">Jun 04, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="[P]" class="user" itemprop="url" name="[P]" href="/user/show/40516522-p">[P]</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781852427269" href="/book/show/214598.Pedro_P_ramo">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40516522-p?shelf=bitchin">bitchin</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1298903565" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9560592131648236776">[P] came out of the airport and into the stifling heat of a Mexican midsummer. Overhead, birds as big as cats circled slowly as though tired of the day. By the exit, a line of black taxi cabs dozed. [P] rapped on the window of the closest cab and waited, his forehead already damp with sweat. The cab slowly pulled away as though trying to free itself from something thick and sticky. An identical cab shuffled forward to take its place. [P] approached, and the window came down erratically, like the</span>
  <span id="freeText9560592131648236776" style="display:none">[P] came out of the airport and into the stifling heat of a Mexican midsummer. Overhead, birds as big as cats circled slowly as though tired of the day. By the exit, a line of black taxi cabs dozed. [P] rapped on the window of the closest cab and waited, his forehead already damp with sweat. The cab slowly pulled away as though trying to free itself from something thick and sticky. An identical cab shuffled forward to take its place. [P] approached, and the window came down erratically, like the movements of a large spider with a missing leg. <br />‘Get in, señor,’ said a voice from inside.<br />[P] gripped the handle, which sent a shock of electric heat through his hand. <br />‘Son of a…’<br />‘Get in.’<br />Hot as Hell. Leather seats clinging to his back and his legs. <br />‘Where to, señor?’<br />‘Comala.’<br />The driver did not turn around, but laughed into the windscreen. <br />‘To Comala? This is your first time in Mexico, no? You do not want to go to Comala, señor.’<br />‘Why not?’<br />‘It is, how you say, <i>dead</i> there? Very few people. For a tourist, you understand.’<br />‘I’m not a tourist. I’m here to write’<br />‘Escritor, señor? Ah, you are a journalist?’<br />‘No, I’m writing a review. Research. Comprende?’<br />‘Si, si. And what are you researching, señor?’<br />[P] ran his hand across his brow. It felt like burning sand. <br />‘Pedro Paramo. You know it? By Juan Rulfo.’<br />‘Si. The action takes place in Comala, no? I understand. You like this book, yes?’<br />‘Naturally. It’s a favourite of mine.’<br />‘It’s a book about a quest. Juan Preciado, his mother she have died, no? And he have promised her to find his father, Pedro Paramo, and make him pay. You are on a quest, to Comala to find Pedro Paramo too. Is clever.’<br />‘Well, yes, I guess. It is a kind of mystery too. There are two questions at the heart of the narrative. One is ‘what is happening in Comala?’ and the other is ‘who is Pedro Paramo?’’<br />‘Living bile.’<br />‘Yes. Abundio, I think, describes Pedro, within the first few pages, as living bile and you read on wanting to know what he has done to deserve it.’ <br />‘Si.’<br />‘And what you find out is that he was a powerful, brutal man, who appropriated land and murdered people.’<br />‘Don’t put that in your review, señor. Is spoiler, no?’<br />‘I guess. But you would want to explore that, right? Because it is important. The unscrupulous business man, who holds a village in the palm his hand, and eventually crushes it. Rulfo wants to make a point about what life was like in these places, about the corruption, the immorality, the exploitation. Pedro Paramo is, amongst other things, a political novel; Paramo actually means 'barren plain.''<br />'I know that, señor.'<br />[P] mopped his brow again.<br />'It's awfully hot in here. Haven't you got any air conditioning?'<br />'Is hot everywhere in Mexico.'<br />'I see. Well, anyway, the book is a kind of Shakespearean tragedy about power and control and poverty…’<br />‘And family too, no?’<br />‘Yes, and family too. Abundio says to Juan Preciado that ‘we are all Pedro Paramo’s sons.’ Or something like that. And he means it figuratively, of course, in that Paramo is the patriarch of the village, the overlord, but he means it literally too, for Pedro fathered many of the inhabitants. One of these children he decided to recognise, Miguel, almost as a wager with Father Renteria. And he, Miguel, turns out maybe even worse than Paramo himself.’<br />‘The sins of the father are passed to the son, señor.’ <br />‘Sin is central to the novel, actually. I don’t know anything about Rulfo’s religious beliefs, or even if he had any, but his book could certainly be interpreted as a comment on, a criticism of Catholicism. Everyone in Comala has sinned; but they are poor and cannot pay for the masses, for the absolution that would save their souls or the souls of their loved ones. These people are beyond saving, seems to be the idea.’<br />‘Mexico is a superstitious, a religious place, señor. The grip is strong. Father Renteria, he is interesting, no?’<br />‘I think so. He is the one who can give absolution. He takes money from Paramo to absolve his son, to give his son forgiveness and, in essence, allow him into heaven. This son, who committed atrocious crimes. Crucially, Renteria himself asks for absolution from a Priest, and is denied it.’<br />Suddenly the taxi came to a halt. [P] was thrown forward. <br />‘We’re here, señor,’ said the driver.<br />‘Uh? What? Where?’<br />‘Comala.’<br /><br /><i>‘Where are you, señor?’<br />‘Comala.’<br />‘You know better than that, [P].’<br />‘How do you know my name?’</i><br /><br />Comala. The earth scorched his feet through the melting rubber soles of his shoes. The landscape seemed to glisten, to move and slide away before his eyes, like images seen in a puddle of oil. <i>You must go to Comala to research your review…</i> <br />‘So this is Comala?’ said [P] to the burro driver.<br />‘You know better than that. This is a ghost town, señor.’<br />‘Ah, yes, si, it is quiet. And <i>hot</i>.’<br />‘Hotter than Hell, señor?’<br />‘I’ve never been…ah, what’s your name?’<br />‘Gabriel Garcia Marquez.’<br />‘You don’t say! Listen, I’m…’<br />‘You’re looking for Pedro Paramo.’<br />‘Yes, I guess. I’m here…’<br />‘To conduct research for a review.’<br />‘How do you know all these things?’<br />‘I spoke to your mother.’ <br />‘My mother is dead.’<br />‘Si.’<br /><br />Doña Gloriana opened the door of her little hut and ushered [P] inside.<br />‘I’ve been waiting for you,’ she said.<br />‘How can that be?’<br />‘Gabriel said that you were on your way.’<br />‘But I was just with him.’<br />‘Si. You look tired, [P].’ <br />‘Where am I?’<br />‘Comala, señor.’<br />‘No.’<br /><br /><i>His arms and face so sunburnt they looked like raw meat...</i> <br /><br />‘Have you read Pedro Paramo, Doña Gloriana?’<br />‘Of course, everyone in Comala has read that book.’<br />‘There is a suggestion, early on, that Comala is Hell.’<br />‘Si.’<br />‘First of all, as he enters Comala Juan Preciado is accompanied by a guide.’ <br />‘Like Virgil in Dante’s Inferno, no?’<br />‘Si. Exactly. And the way to the village is described as always down, always descending. And there’s the intense heat, of course.’<br />‘Of course. Si. But don’t think about these things, my son; sleep, rest.’<br />‘All the inhabitants of Comala, the people Juan Preciado meets, are deceased; I think everyone picks up on that eventually. But they are also in purgatory. That became clear to me. They cannot absolve themselves of sin, they are, then, ghosts, or souls, trapped, in an intermediary stage, between Heaven and Hell…they cannot buy their release. Life in a poor Mexican village is a kind of purgatory; maybe that was the point.’ <br /><i>Shhh, [P], go to sleep, my son…</i><br /><br />In the dark room [P] could hear chattering voices. Echoes of the past.<br />‘Who is there?’ he shouted.<br /><i>We are here. Who said that? I did. Let him speak! Tell him. He came all this way to research his review. To Comala, for that? Si. And he has spoken to Gabriel, the taxi driver, and Doña Gloriana, and never once mentioned the voices. That’s us! Si, si. Pedro Paramo is polyphonic. Did he not say anything at all about that? No, señor. It is composed of multiple voices, echoes of the past. So many voices vying for attention. He hasn’t spoken about the structure, either. Shut it. Let him speak, goddamn it! The narrative is jumbled, as though it were a painting that someone has cut into tiny pieces and thrown all over the floor. So you have to pick up the pieces and put them together again? Si.</i> <br />‘Be quiet, all of you. Let him have his rest.’<br /><i>Can the dead rest, Doña Gloriana?</i><br />‘Si. Si. If they are good boys.’</span>
  <a data-text-id="9560592131648236776" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Diana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/89242656-diana"><img alt="Diana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565726515p2/89242656.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3529732851?book_show_action=true">Sep 14, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Diana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Diana" href="/user/show/89242656-diana">Diana</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/89242656-diana?shelf=latin-american-lit">latin-american-lit</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3529732851" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1786823466854354022">Pedro Páramo [1955/83] by Juan Rulfo - ★★★★<br/><br/>"A simplicity and profundity worthy of Greek tragedy...Wuthering Heights located in Mexico and written by Kafka", The Guardian<br/><br/>This book influenced Gabriel García Márquez in the writing of One Hundred Years of Solitude, as well as opened a path for so many other “magical realism” books to come. In Pedro Páramo, our protagonist Juan Preciado arrives to Comala, Mexico, to meet his father, Pedro Páramo, whom he has never met before. What the narrator finds </span>
  <span id="freeText1786823466854354022" style="display:none">Pedro Páramo [1955/83] by Juan Rulfo - ★★★★<br /><br />"A simplicity and profundity worthy of Greek tragedy...Wuthering Heights located in Mexico and written by Kafka", The Guardian<br /><br />This book influenced Gabriel García Márquez in the writing of One Hundred Years of Solitude, as well as opened a path for so many other “magical realism” books to come. In Pedro Páramo, our protagonist Juan Preciado arrives to Comala, Mexico, to meet his father, Pedro Páramo, whom he has never met before. What the narrator finds is one almost completely abandoned village with strange-behaving inhabitants who tell him contradictory things. From these first pages, we are in the realm of mystery and eeriness as the worlds of ghosts and the living merge, and the collective memory and objective history start to take different paths. Time becomes indeterminate, the story jumps periods, but at the heart of it all is still one man - Pedro Páramo, whose thirst for power and the dream to realise one of his deepest and most private desires soon signals the downfall for the entire village. The book is not long, but it has the feel of a far-reaching and deep family saga that mixes wonderfully myth and reality, past, present and future, and spirituality and realism – a true Mexican classic.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1786823466854354022" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2686141595" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2686141595" />
    <a title="·Karen·" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1652898-karen"><img alt="·Karen·" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1434742215p2/1652898.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2686141595?book_show_action=true">Feb 23, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="·Karen·" class="user" itemprop="url" name="·Karen·" href="/user/show/1652898-karen">·Karen·</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781781253168" href="/book/show/22931012-pedro-p-ramo">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2686141595" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer692251451950677899"><i>'This world presses in on us from every side; it scatters fistfuls of our dust across the land and takes bits and pieces of us as if to water the earth with our blood. What did we do? Why have our souls rotted away? Your mother always said that at the very least we could count on God's mercy. Yet you deny it, Susana. Why do you deny me as your father? Are you mad?'<br/>'Didn't you know?'<br/>'Are you mad?'<br/>'Of course I am, Bartolomé. Didn't you know?'</i><br/><br/>Of course Susana is mad, and of course the dead are not </span>
  <span id="freeText692251451950677899" style="display:none"><i>'This world presses in on us from every side; it scatters fistfuls of our dust across the land and takes bits and pieces of us as if to water the earth with our blood. What did we do? Why have our souls rotted away? Your mother always said that at the very least we could count on God's mercy. Yet you deny it, Susana. Why do you deny me as your father? Are you mad?'<br />'Didn't you know?'<br />'Are you mad?'<br />'Of course I am, Bartolomé. Didn't you know?'</i><br /><br />Of course Susana is mad, and of course the dead are not at peace. They sleep but fitfully, woken by moaning and murmuring. Intrigued and troubled by voices they hear.<br /><br /><i>' When you hear her again, let me know. I'd like to know what she's saying.'<br />'You hear? I think she's about to say something. I hear a kind of murmuring.' <br />'No, that isn't her. That's farther away and in the other direction. And that's a man's voice. What happens with these corpses that have been dead a long time is that when the damp reaches them they begin to stir. They wake up.'</i><br /><br />A narrative like a delirious dream, one that cannot sustain a structure, but that creates an atmosphere. Loss, delusion, harsh and barren landscapes (I am informed that Páramo in Spanish means barren plain, wasteland), death that does not bring peace or resolution or relief from suffering. The suffering plays out over generations, is compounded and magnified and repeated. Nothing can provide redemption, not love, not the priest, not a rebellion against the powerful for that rebellion is corrupted and deflected by the powerful.<br /><br />The afterword by Susan Sontag tells me that Rulfo himself called this novel an exercise in elimination. 'The practice of writing the short stories disciplined me, and made me see the need to disappear and leave my characters the freedom to talk at will, which provoked, it would seem, a lack of structure. Yes, there is structure in <i>Pedro Páramo</i> but it is a structure made of silences, of hanging threads, of cut scenes, where everything occurs in a simultaneous time which is a no-time.' <br /><br />Takes a little concentration, then. Not for those who want a linear story. The simultaneous time, the no-time, that distresses our deep need for cogent narrative, and we slip from first person to third and back again without quite knowing who is speaking, or who is 'he', but with a bit of effort even story will emerge, that too will emerge. And more, much more. It's worth reading three times.</span>
  <a data-text-id="692251451950677899" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1958079448" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1958079448" />
    <a title="Teresa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/541416-teresa"><img alt="Teresa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1570841350p2/541416.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1958079448?book_show_action=true">Mar 31, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Teresa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Teresa" href="/user/show/541416-teresa">Teresa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1958079448" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1521969738634565116">The blurb on the back of this book states that Rulfo’s style is what would come to be known as “magical realism.” To me, it read as a ghost story: a journey into a ghost town, literally populated with ghosts. The story of the town is passed on, like a baton in a relay race, from one dead to another: Each recognizes the other is dead, but some seem slow to realize they are too.<br/><br/>I read this alongside Hamsun’s <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18890002.Growth_of_the_Soil' title='Growth of the Soil by Knut Hamsun' rel='nofollow noopener'>Growth of the Soil</a> (which I’m still reading) and while it’s of a different time period (an</span>
  <span id="freeText1521969738634565116" style="display:none">The blurb on the back of this book states that Rulfo’s style is what would come to be known as “magical realism.” To me, it read as a ghost story: a journey into a ghost town, literally populated with ghosts. The story of the town is passed on, like a baton in a relay race, from one dead to another: Each recognizes the other is dead, but some seem slow to realize they are too.<br /><br />I read this alongside Hamsun’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18890002.Growth_of_the_Soil" title="Growth of the Soil by Knut Hamsun" rel="nofollow noopener">Growth of the Soil</a> (which I’m still reading) and while it’s of a different time period (and country), I was struck by the barrenness of the land in this novella (in stark contrast to the richness of the other), all due to the tyranny of one family, especially the <i>patron</i> of the title, and the family's selfish lusts.<br /><br />I can only imagine how wonderful this must be in its original language.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1521969738634565116" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('w+qte9VqinaPiDJBmLznH8LPZghXXlijQ1aqys48TwPyeJBgNqK7B9ENzDRcg6qhGk5mdpNBhLk9GUu6/nG24g==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('99w3ITfdmuirTHna6Wrrm5cz79Zpwho0umFB0nqMUcrGTgo61BWrmfXJh68tVaYlT7LvqK3dxi7ELqCiSsGoKw==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

Por último, Cuando salí de la Habana, válgame Dios recrea el tema ancestral de los viajes en el tiempo para demostrar que no hay escape posible contra el peso de la historia en nuestra existencia individual.\n\nAparecido por primera vez en 1972 y reeditado constantemente desde entonces, El principio del placer se ofrece ahora en una nueva versión que lo hace el mismo libro y a la vez otro nuevo, enriquecido con toda clase de precisiones que lo vuelven aún más nítido y coherente.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/21778.Juan_Rulfo">About Juan Rulfo</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/21778.Juan_Rulfo"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1226313372p3/21778.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/21778.Juan_Rulfo">Juan Rulfo
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
1,039
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer9035537469676956646">Juan Rulfo nació el 16 de mayo de 1917 Él sostuvo que esto ocurrió en la casa familiar de Apulco, Jalisco, aunque fue registrado en la ciudad de Sayula, donde se conserva su acta de nacimiento. Vivió en la pequeña población de San Gabriel, pero las tempranas muertes de su padre, primero (1923), y de su madre poco después (1927), obligaron a sus familiares a inscribirlo en un internado en Guadalaja</span>
  <span id="freeText9035537469676956646" style="display:none">Juan Rulfo nació el 16 de mayo de 1917 Él sostuvo que esto ocurrió en la casa familiar de Apulco, Jalisco, aunque fue registrado en la ciudad de Sayula, donde se conserva su acta de nacimiento. Vivió en la pequeña población de San Gabriel, pero las tempranas muertes de su padre, primero (1923), y de su madre poco después (1927), obligaron a sus familiares a inscribirlo en un internado en Guadalajara, la capital del estado de Jalisco.<br /><br />Durante sus años en San Gabriel entró en contacto con la biblioteca de un cura (básicamente literaria), depositada en la casa familiar, y recordará siempre estas lecturas, esenciales en su formación literaria. Algunos acostumbran destacar su temprana orfandad como determinante en su vocación artística, olvidando que su conocimiento temprano de los libros mencionados tendría un peso mayor en este terreno.<br /><br />Una huelga de la Universidad de Guadalajara le impide inscribirse en ella y decide trasladarse a la ciudad de México. La imposibilidad de revalidar los estudios hechos en Jalisco tampoco le permite ingresar a la Universidad Nacional, pero asiste como oyente a los cursos de historia del arte de la Facultad de Filosofía y Letras. Se convierte así en un conocedor muy serio de la bibliografía histórica, antropológica y geográfica de México, temas que un estudio minucioso de su obra literaria y fotográfica permite rastrear en las mismas, además de los textos y la labor editorial que les dedicó. Durante buena parte de las décadas de 1930 y 1940 viaja extensamente por el país, trabaja en Guadalajara o en la ciudad de México y a partir de 1945 comienza a publicar sus cuentos en dos revistas: América, de la capital, y Pan, de Guadalajara. La primera de ellas significa su confirmación como escritor, gracias al apoyo de su gran amigo Efrén Hernández. Publica sus imágenes por primera vez, también en América, en 1949. Pero fue a finales de la década de 1930 cuando se iniciaba como escritor y fotógrafo, aunque pocos sabían de esto.<br /><br />A mediados de los cuarenta da comienzo también su relación amorosa con Clara Aparicio, de la que queda el testimonio epistolar (publicado en 2000 en Aire de las colinas. Cartas a Clara). Se casa con ella en 1948 y los hijos aumentarán la familia poco a poco. Abandona su trabajo en una empresa fabricante de neumáticos a principios de los cincuenta y obtiene en 1952 la primera de dos becas consecutivas (1952-1953 y 1953-1954) que le otorga el Centro Mexicano de Escritores, fundado por la estadounidense Margaret Shedd, quien fue sin duda la persona determinante para que Rulfo publicase en 1953 El Llano en llamas (donde reúne siete cuentos ya publicados en América e incorpora otros ocho, nuevos) y, en 1955, Pedro Páramo (novela de la que publicó tres adelantos en 1954, en las revistas Las letras patrias, Universidad de México y Dintel). En 1958 termina de escribir su segunda novela (muy breve), El gallo de oro, que no se publicará hasta 1980. En 2010 aparece la edición definitiva de esta última obra, después de una revisión cuidadosa del original que permitió eliminar errores e inconsistencias de la versión previamente conocida.<br /><br />A partir de la publicación de los dos primeros títulos el prestigio literario de Rulfo habrá de incrementarse de manera constante, hasta convertirse en el escritor mexicano más reconocido en México y el extranjero. Entre sus admiradores se cuentan Mario Benedetti, José María Arguedas, Carlos Fuentes, Jorge Luis Borges, Gabriel García Márquez, Günter Grass, Susan Sontag, Elias Canetti, Tahar Ben Jelloun, Urs Widmer, Gao Xingjian, Kenzaburo Oe, Enrique Vila-Matas y muchos otros. Murió el 7 de enero de 1986.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9035537469676956646" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
